RATING: 7
I have  soft spot in my heart for vikings, yet had yet to find a viking themed game that I really love. Then I met this game, adequately named "Vikings". It's worker placement, and tile laying, and solitaire packaged in a point generating versus game - but I like it. It's simple and elegant and comes with an additional expansion you can add in when the vanilla game gets stale. For me, this is one I love to play every year.

RATING: 7.5
Vikings is a lighter game with limited player interaction.  That's not to say it's not there: you are looking at your opponents' board to see if they'll take what you want, or likewise so you might take what they need.  You need to decide *when* to pull the trigger on that tasty tile up ahead, weighing it's cost vs the risk of losing it entirely.

Aside from that, Vikings is largely an efficiency game of building your own board with the tiles present this round.  You look forward to see which ships are out that might knock your pieces down, try to obtain the ones you want, and generally just try to play well.

This game fits into my collection as a title with limited, non-aggressive player interaction that's light and fairly short.  It's fairly peaceful, but you still need to mind your opponents so it's definitely not multiplayer solitaire.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Accessible, attractive, with deep decision-making, but easy-to-pick-up rules, I can see why BGG users are clamouring to get hold of this out-of-print tile-laying classic. Everything is so streamlined and intuitive, you might expect the game to be a breeze. But there's a real challenge here, and it's an enjoyable one.

RATING: 10
Vikings is maybe the best lightweight game with a twist. It's easy to setup, easy to explain, fast playing and has a high replay value. Also it scales very well. It has a good mix of strategy and tactics. It's a good gateway game for new gamers and also a great filler for veterans.

Vikings also has an advanced mode (called The Progress Version) that ads more depth to the game. We always play this variant, because it gives you more strategic choices.

RATING: 8
2-4(3)

The progress version really ramps it up. Having boatsmen only move a single viking really is better and the special tiles are all pretty interesting in their own right and really motivate players to spend up a little bit. I'm not sure I want the hassle of an auction variant, but everything else is good.

It's fast, with an interesting and meaningful spatial element and interesting decisions as far as how far to go for your chosen pairing. Interesting that a big money and a tight money strategy are both viable (though you have to be really creative and accommodating if you play with little money).

Money is very useful, but I've found that nobles can really be a big factor. Just a really, really solid game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
Elegant design by Michael Kiesling, you place tiles on a mainland grid which you've bought from a wheel-shaped offer area, and each round begets a particular type of scoring. The placement of tiles is imperative to scoring and warding off the threats from Viking ships, which are also a way to score Fame and gold. In fact, the scoring of this games is one of its strengths, and made even better with the special tiles used in the advanced game, which I would not play without because they add just the right level of depth to take it beyond a gateway. However, the auction variant can slow the game down significantly, despite the strategies it adds. I think it can be left out without losing much of the feel, unless you want a longer session.

RATING: 6.5
A rather good bidding game (even if the bidding part is not that apparent). It's got a nice amount of tension, and it plays fast.

UPDATE: After a few plays at yucata.de, I've come to the conclusion that I really enjoy this game, and it will probably become part of my wishlist quite soon.

UPDATE: I'm beginning to get a bit tired of this one. It's a great design, and the price wheel is just brilliant. But on the downside, it feels just like most drafting games... you can either be lucky enough that other players don't need what you need, or unlucky enough to get everything you could possibly use strip away from you.

It's a better 2-player game than it is 3 or 4 player, as this gets mitigated, though. For 2 players, I still rate it an 8... but as I usually game with more people, I must downgrade it's rating to a 7.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
First play and initial impressions were good; rules were quick to learn, the mechanic is original, there appeared to be depth and the opportunity for various strategies. But as the game progressed the significant randomness of the tile draw and figure allocation soon revealed this to be a game with a significant reliance on blind luck. Draw the islands that face the 'wrong way' and future turns are utterly scuppered through no fault of your own, just because the next few turns don't throw up compatible shapes. Often my turn had only one option owing to the island tile configuration. Meanwhile your opponant can't believe their luck being swamped with choice.

In my game the winner had confessed to having no idea what he was doing during the 6 rounds but ended up with navigators by default. The last turn the tile draw fell in his lap and he walked it.

I had a feeling of helplessness throughout, playing the optimum from what was available but feeling that the game was dictating what I should do rather than having any scope for me shaping my strategy. No wish to play again.

RATING: 7.75
This feel of this game reminds me of something else, but I can't quite put my finger on what. After only one game, I can see that every game would be very different because of the tiles and vikings that come up each round. However, I wonder if the randomness of it would become tiring after many plays. You can't really stick to a long-term strategy because you don't know what tiles/vikings will be available. It's definitely more tactical in that way. There are lots of decisions to make, though, trying to figure out what the best move is for you, whether it's worth the amount that you'd have to pay for it now or if it's worth taking the chance that the same stuff will be available and cheaper on your next turn, whether it's better to take something that would help one of your opponents more--stuff like that. I definitely want to play it again.

UPDATE: It scales well from 2-4, but they feel quite different. With two players you get stuck with half the boats that come out, so it's a real struggle to get the placements right so that you get all your points and money.

RATING: 6.5
Initial rating after 2 consecutive 2p games (basic version online at BSW).  Seems like a pretty good abstract with room to manipulate other players... but sometimes it's most important to avoid hurting yourself with "bad" plays (like having no place to play a "middle" island piece).  Luck of the draw could be significant sometimes, but there are enough ways to mitigate luck if you get creative and stay flexible... plus the game plays pretty quickly so the luck doesn't bother me.

** UPDATE: With >2 players, this game is very different.  I got crushed in a 3p game because the game moves along much faster, and you get fewer tiles.  Also, I probably spent too much gold in the early rounds... I think money is supposed to be very tight such that buying something more than 5 gold is extravagant.  It's still an intriguing game right now, but the rating could fall if additional 3-4p games fail to impress me.

(3 VERSIONS EXIST- basic, advanced & BSW).  BSW has only the basic version with a slight modification that all tiles get shifted to the lowest position when the zero tile is taken (compared to the pricing wheel of the "real", printed board game).  The advanced or "progress" game has an auction for start player each round (and a slightly weird setup of tiles & vikings)... but it also has 25 bonus tiles that players obtain by buying the highest value set at a given moment (except the last tile/viking... the one taken for zero).  The bonus tiles are scoring bonus only... not additional land tiles... but they give you more to think about and maybe balance out some luck that can lead to big 7 & 10 point swings.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.6
Slick game once you get to start playing it. We played it in 45 minutes, but probably overcooked the rules explanation upfront and that took 15 minutes or so! I really like the rotary mechanism for the money where you have to buy a tile/viking if there is more than one available, rather than taking 1 for free. If there's nothing left to buy, then the dial turns round to set the next available tile to zero value. This is easier to see than to explain, it's a really novel mechanic I've not seen before and it makes the games strategic calculations interesting.

I really enjoyed this game after 1 play. I had pretty much 3 of every role at the end, only 2 ships to threaten me and I won by about 20 points. This game worked out well for me but I would have been keen to play this again, regardless of the result.

I've since played this again with more in depth rules, which I didn't really understand beforehand, but the facts are that this is still a good game and the fact that there are a number of levels of complexity mean that you can grow in to the game as long as everyones reading from the same page!

I've played this game a few more times now and reckon that I understand all the rules, It's a great game!

RATING: 8

RATING: 8.7
Your running a Viking outpost.  Your goal is to balance how various members balance you settlement and also empower it.  Settle islands and decide which combination of islands you settle for the most points.

This is a very interesting little game that plays in about 45 minutes.  The components are well made and attractive.  The rules are easy to understand and a set of tiles are included to make the game more interesting if you tire of the basic game.

Some of the planning for various tiles and such can have a littrle bit of screwage but it is not overwhelming.

RATING: 6
First Play: 3/15/2008, Beans Game Day
Impressions after one play: (6)
Cute game with an interesting bidding and purchasing mechanic driven mosty by the 'Wheel Of Vikings'.
Balance is key, and not all scoring opportunities were fully understood in first play through (especially the massive penalty for not properly feeding everyone at the end of the game.
Decisions seem slightly constrained, there are very few total decision points in the game, summarized by:
 Value to bid for first player token (6 opportunities)
 Which color group of vikings to place on the wheel (6 opportunities)
 Which viking/tile combination to pay for (12/#players opportunities per game turn, 6 game turns)
 Which bonus tile to take (if applicable, max 4/game turn for all players)
 Where to place 'stranded' vikings with boatmen (6 opportunities at end of each game turn)

Initial over-empahsis placed on gaining access to special tiles.
Would probably be willing to play this again, but would not seek to add it to my collection.

Upon review, looks like we played some of the rules very wrong - so this is worth another look.

RATING: 7
I don't like the structure of this game: simple placement rules (relatively), but then hideously complex and unintuitive scoring. It's like Carcassonne without the elegance; imagine if Carc had five different types of meeples, which scored in different ways at five different times. Still, I can see that there's a germ of a good game here, once the scoring is internalized, and I'll revisit it later to see if my initial impression is perhaps too harsh.

UPDATE: With a better understanding of the scoring, this game is more fun. It's still clunky though, and frankly luck of the draw seems huge.

RATING: 6
My previous play on this was the "basic" game; I finally had a chance to play the "auction" version, and was not much impressed. Because the boatmen are much less powerful, there seems like there is a much larger premium on making sure guys get played when bought and don't sit around waiting for transport. But there just isn't enough control in the purchasing phase, and you are too often screwed for no good reason (because you can't buy the '0'-cost chit under some circumstances, you can be put in the horrible position of being forced to pay large amounts of cash for tiles you simply don't want, or having the only tile you want be the only one you can't purchase at any price).

Anyway, despite this, I felt like it was still a reasonably clever game. But there is just too much better stuff out there.
---

RATING: 4
Updated 5/13/10.

Probably best two-player.  I bought Vikings to be a light, non-confrontational weeknight filler to play with my wife.  It usually does the trick for us.  It's not super deep (we play the base game, not the advanced version), but with repeated plays the decisions do seem to get more agonizing.  It's not the best game ever (the art and colors are downright childish), but it makes us happy.  And happiness should count for something.  

I think it's a great Euro sampler, too. It requires just enough awareness of what your opponent is doing, some indirect conflict, some resource management, some spacial awareness, and finally some abstract-esque planning (If I purchase A now, then s/he will purchase B, the wheel will turn, and I won't be able to purchase C, so I'll be stuck with D, so I should purchase C now, even though it's more expensive, right?).  

After many, many plays, however, I'm starting to find it a bit stale.  In roughly the same time frame, my wife and I could play 2-player Homesteaders, 2-player Vasco da Gama, or 2-player Hansa Teutonica, all of which have significantly more depth of strategy.  I'd be open to trying the advanced version to see if adds anything, but this has become a game that only comes off the shelf now when (1) we're too burnt-out for anything complicated, but are looking for more than a 15-minute card game; or (2) we're introducing Euro games to non-gamers.

RATING: 4
The price wheel mechanism is an interesting idea but, in this game, it doesn't offer much in the way of interesting choices.  Too often, the difference in value between the best tile choice and second best tile choice is so huge that there is no strategy or tactic involved; just choose the obviously best tile.  Since the presention of tiles is random, it is also random whether or not if you will get a chance to make a meaningful decision.

Very unsatisfying although there is a decision to make about how to lay your tile in your play area.  In my mind, this is a convoluted Alhambra.

Did I mention that it is fiddly to set up and maintain during play, and the scoring rounds have up to 11 different scoring methods?  Given the Byzantine scoring tables this should have been themed with a Turkish motif.

RATING: 6
BOTTOM LINE: vikings is a fantastic family/new gamer game. It also offers alot for the experienced gamer. Very fun easy game with lots of hard choices. 


My criterion for ranking games


http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/147053/item/2300390#item2300390


1) Sense of wonder:    Each game has a great deal of mystery just in the tiles/vikings and which ones will come out and which ones be paired together. I eagerly anticipate the combos and change my strategy each time based on these factors

2) Meaningful/fun decisions.   Great agony over which vikings to buy and which to hope wheels to you. 

3) Player interaction:  There is some interaction in this game as you have to care about what you opponents are doing and how it may affect you. 

4) Components/artwork:  Very nice artwork, great components, tiles are nice. 

5) Game length/complexity:  Very quick game and easy to explain. The game lasts the perfect amount of time that you can play 3 or 4 in a row.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game embodies a lot of what makes for a traditional Euro game. It has a fairly simple set of rules. The overall game doesn't take very long. The individual turns don't take very long either and there are interesting decisions to make. The theme is pretty thin but that's not a huge drawback for me because I like interesting mechanics and the theme works well enough. 

This game, with experienced players, is the definition of what one may term a Euro-filler game (i.e. a game that can be played in about a half hour and still provide the players with some interesting situations to think about). The player interaction is manifested in a successive set of random, limited markets with market values of items potentially being influenced by the choices of other players. It's very Euro in that your decisions affect other players but you're not "attacking" them (which is kind of funny given the artwork on the box and the general conotations one might derive from such imagery - though the artwork does fit the idea of trying to defend one's self from invaders which is part of the theme).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I dig games with a bit of per-player spatial positioning (e.g. Alhambra, PoF). Trying to get your islands situated just right without "wasting" too many vikings (placing on the canoe) is challenging and fun. There's probably not quite as much choice in the vikings/tiles wheel as it first appears, since money is very tight you're often forced to just pick the cheapest thing (or at least on my first play it seemed that way). But we also learned that just taking cheap stuff can make island placement a real pain. The only player interaction is in choosing which viking/tile pair to buy, but you could probably take more advantage of the information on other player boards than I did. But also, I think the advanced version, which I haven't tried yet, adds more player interaction. I look forward to trying that. Only 1 game so far. Rating could drop if advanced rules don't measure up.

RATING: N/A
$23 BGG
Vikings is a fast economic game. Despite the nominal "Viking" theme, no actual exploration or pillaging is involved. The resources in the game consist of coins and several types of ship tiles, island tiles and meeples. In each of 6 rounds, a random set of 12 tiles and 12 meeples becomes available. Players take turns buying and placing pairs of meeples and tiles. There is no direct player interaction, only indirect contention for resources during the buying phase. The most unusual aspect of the game is the pricing wheel, which pairs meeples with tiles and sets their prices.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
If I weren't such a curmudgeon, I might bump this one to a 9. The game never disappoints, and everyone I've introduced it to has liked it. The game has many familiar euro mechanisms. You have to keep the need to feed your people in the back of your mind; you get multiple scoring options, including creating the most of this or the largest of that; you use the Thurn & Taxis "all-of-one-color or one-of each-color" placement rule for one facet of the game; etc.
Nevertheless, the game feels unique. Essentially, players take turns choosing a set to buy from among a dwindling number of sets at different prices. Each set consists of a meeple and a board tile. Each player uses what he has bought to build his individual board and to place his meeples on it to produce an optimal score during the scoring rounds.

RATING: 8
Starting this out at an 8 because I really enjoyed this game.  However it reminds me a little of St Petersburg.  I also started that one out at 8 but it has dropped w/repeat plays.  This one does seem to have a little more to it though.  Each turn you buy an island tile and a worker (color).  You wind up placing these items on your game board.  They score you either money or vps.  The best part of the game is deciding what to buy.  There is a wheel that determines the price.  Some items are very expensive, but the cost can come down if you are willing to risk waiting on them.  I think though after 1 play, I'm ready for the advanced version.  I'm hopeful that it will add different strategies to the game.  As it stands now, I feel that the games may be a bit formulaic...buy yellow early, pick up blues when you can...pick greens/greys/blacks next depending on situation...last, buy reds.

After 6 plays, dropping this down to a 7.  I still really like it, but in the games I've won, I've had the most farmers.  In the games I've lost, another player has had more than me.  I think too much of it comes down to who has the most farmers at game's end.

I just won a game with 2 farmers while all other players had 3.  Gotta bump it back up to an 8.

The expansion (25 extra tiles) plus bidding for start player was fun even though I got demolished.  I'd like to play it that way from now on...not the demolished part though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Players are recruiting different skilled vikings and installing them on an islands to earn money and victory points.  Vikings and chunks of island are purchased off a pricing wheel, which gradually cheapens the offerings.  Vikings can be nobles (earn points), scouts (earn points for other vikings), goldsmiths (earn money), fisherman (feed the people) and so on.  Islands are created, Carcassonne style, and the vikings are placed onto them, meeple style, to trigger their abilities.  As a game, it works well with some nice clever systems.  However, the theme seems awfully misplaced here.  Still, rock solid offering (as always) from Hans im Glueck.

A 2015 re-visit with a four player game, not so good.  Too few options, and luck played a huge part - if you were sitting in the right seat when the pieces are revealed, you're golden.  Else you're rust.

RATING: 8
A very good tile-purchasing, pawn-placing game with a puzzle-y aspect to it.   Completely themeless, in fact the pasted-on theme doesn't even make sense, but as an abstract tile game it works surprisingly well.  The devaluation of tiles via the price wheel is clever and somewhat agonizing as taking a cheap tile could cause a big drop in prices for everybody else.   The spatial element of the tile placement offers a pretty simple puzzle to work out, combined with the risk and timing issues of not always getting what you want when you want it.   Those elements of the game are well-balanced without going over the top.    Enjoyable game.

RATING: 6
Many systems effectively create tension by delaying the satisfaction of goals. There is some worthwhile interaction on the wheel and in endgame majority scoring, but it's mostly about optimizing your own board. Two problems I encountered: (1) If you close off too many islands too early, you'll find yourself with no way to extend islands in the late game, which leads to unsatisfying wasted turns. (2) There's little to generate ambiguity in the last round, so it slows down as players make anti-climactic maximizing calculations.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
When you think of a game with a Viking theme, you're thinking of pillaging, plundering, discovery, and probably sailing.  Those ideas are paper thin in Vikings, but it's still a good game to play if you like Euros and especially if you typically have a player count of 3.  This is very much a tile laying game and it's strange that you would use money to buy land tiles and vikings with different functions.  The purchasing mechanism is very interesting in that you can take a tile and viking for free if you meet certain conditions, but when you do, you are making tiles cheaper for everyone else.  It plays pretty quickly and has good decisions to be made which I always appreciate in a game. One criticism seems to be that if you let someone hoard all of the fishermen (blue meeples), they will likely win. It's probably just a matter of letting players know beforehand.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I must say that this game works better if the island- and ship tiles are laid visible to the board BEFORE the auction phase. More blood, more fun. That is against the game rules but sometimes it's better to find own ways.
--------
We made another home rule in my group: We used boatsmen like in basic game rules but we also used the special tiles - so we decided that the special tile "boat" is considered as boatsman at final scoring when the most boatsmen are counted (as well as the original tiles with same function, so we had 10 tiles with that function). That's because most of us were frustrated about unjustice of those special boat tiles when played according to original rules.

I think I bother to play this only if the variation I mentioned before the dashed line is used. Another home rule worked well also but it's not totally necessary in my opinion.

RATING: 8
This is quite fun and plays in under an hour (1:15 w/advanced rules).  The tile laying & viking placement is similar to Carcassonne.  The money wheel is pretty slick in how it reduces the cost of remaining items.  But the game REALLY comes alive with the advanced ruleset.  The best part of the game is the tough choice of saving money vs. buying the most expensive item (to get one of the special tiles).  The "perks" these special tiles give, and the expanded purpose of the boat tiles turn this game from an average one to a possible great one.

While I prefer the advanced version, I do think it's a great idea to have a game that is adaptable for families as well as gamers.  They came up with a great solution by converting a "gateway" family game into a strategic "gamer's game" simply by having an expanded ruleset.

Grade: B+  (advanced game)
Grade: C    (basic)

RATING: 6
I generally don't care for tile laying games as there usually isn't enough there to keep me interested, but this one was okay.

I like the rondel mechanism that automatically makes things cheaper as the round goes on coupled with the rule where you can't buy the 0 value meeples unless it's the last of it's color.  It throws a wrinkle into everyone's plans, especially in the later rounds where you are looking for specific tile shapes/meeple colors and so you'll get a 0 value meeple, and then the next one will be 6 or 7 :)

Theme is pasted on, but for some odd reason that doesn't bother me with this game as much as it does with others.

Works great with 3.  With 2 there are too many tiles, and with 4 there are too few.
Around 1 hour, so it doesn't overstay it's welcome, especially if you don't have AP-prone people.

Kétszemélyes "módban" prbóltam ki a játékot. 4-5 játék után azt mondom, hogy ez egy nagyon jó játék. A játék egyetlen hiányossága talán a tematika hangulat hiánya. Tulajdonképpen egy absztrakt játékról van szó, egy érezhetően utólag ráhuzott témával.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
You build islands and place vikings with different professions on them. Nobles generate victory points, gold smiths produce money, etc. Then there are enemy ships which threaten your islands and prevent the vikings on them from doing their jobs unless you also have warriors to fend them off.

The major gimmick of the game is the market wheel where you can buy island parts and vikings. Tiles that have been bought are placed in individual areas so everybody is building for himself. Interaction with other players is indirect at best, and the random drawing of tiles and vikings for the market has quite a bit of impact on the game. The advanced rules fix this problem to some extent since the bonus tiles provide incentives for spending more money and jumping ahead further on the rondel than you would ordinarily have reason to, thus rendering the starting order a bit less important. In any case, the advanced rules improve the game quite a bit and open up more avenues of play.

The theme is certainly something that many people will call nonexistent as there is no sailing, no pillaging, no drinking, no fighting (except in a very abstract manner). If that bothers you, Vikings is probably not for you.

Still, Vikings is a fun game with a rather short play time, and almost no downtime because of the quick shopping rounds.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Vikings should hit on all cylinders: It's a Kiesling game, it's got a unique market mechanic, the vikings have different abilities/values depending on their color, tile-laying is an integral part of the game, and the double threat of the ships and not having enough fisherman to provide food makes for good constraints. And yet, Vikings falls flat for me. 
First off, other than the market, it's more or less multi-player solitaire. Second, I typically like tile-laying games, but I don't get any excitement from building the most  efficient island possible. 
I can see why others would like this game. And I won't say no if someone is keen to play. For me, take it or leave it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Updated again: Upon further review, I decided to move the rating back up a notch. Yes, luck did have something to do with my previous win, but further analysis shows that I was wise to take fishermen early to support my vikings and to concentrate on completing islands for the bonus at the end. Maybe I was lucky, but the other players were kind of dense not to realize their populations were going to count against them in the end.
Updated: I decided to knock this one down a notch because of the experience with the most recent game. I think many people will dislike this game because there's a lot of luck involved in how it plays out (unless, I suppose, you play with the variant auction rules for Viking selection and placement). In the game we just played, I was the least experienced, least knowledgeable player at the table, and I won by a wide margin. I'll do a session report for full details.
This is an enjoyable game of managing multiple logistic elements with a fair amount of chance tossed in. Players must balance the need to land different classes of Vikings while building islands while collecting gold. The chance element may be a bit high for some folks, however. You have to make the most of the opportunities presented.

RATING: 8
A good game, and a very original game design.  The amount of control players have is questionable, but I like the weird constraints that the inventive game mechanisms place on your actions.  The wheel thing that drives the game is a totally contrived, game-y device, but it does an admirable job of regulating the game's economy.  Vikings is definitely a game aimed at gamers, but it will always feel "light" because there's so little ability to plan between game rounds.  You see what the wheel of fortune gives you, and you do the best you can with it. 

We use the special tiles and the advanced rules for ferrymen (1 passenger per ferryman), but haven't yet tried the auction.  It seems like overkill - too much analysis for a game of this weight and length.  For what it's worth, I'll mention that Vikings is kind of a pain in the ass to teach to new players.  There's a lot to explain for a game of its weight and length.  Once you get going, though, it plays effortlessly.  Best with three players.

[Update] After trying the game with the full advanced rules, I have mixed feelings.  I like the ability to throw out a viking and choose the order of the vikings around the wheel.  I don't like the money auction for start player.  Why?  Because it seems like the main thing to be determined is not who gets to go first, but rather who gets to be screwed by going last.  If the player to your left is Mr. Moneybags Perpetual Start Player, you really get screwed.  Also, bidding to start before you even know what tiles will be available just feels . . . dumb.  Next time, I'd like to try it with the advanced viking placement "draft" but with the traditional rotating start player.

RATING: 10
Another one of the very fine games. Sort of similair in actions to Saint Petersburg, where you have to keep a balance between all different characters.

In Vikings you have to balance out the guys that bring you money, bring you food, bring you points and defend you from the viking ships!! And those guys love to attack!! You have got to be on your toes!! 

But it's not all that straightforward, as other players try to do the same thing, so you have to keep a sharp eye on what's going on. Very balanced mechanism between points and money. 

Besides the push and pull of money and points, you also have to keep in mind the many different bonus points awarded at the end of the game, as those will be sometimes your swinging balance points. 

I like this game a lot. Plays quickly, is very fun and not too much of a brain burner. And that makes it very enjoyable in my eyes.

RATING: 8
The price wheel is an interesting system of acquiring tiles and vikings to be placed on them. The fact that one always acquires a tile and a viking together gives the player some hard choices, as tiles must fit in a certain way and vikings of different colors have different functions. The advanced version adds bonus tiles that make evaluation even harder.

On the negative side, the game feels a little abstract, despite some thematic elements. There may be some turn order issues, and I don't think the auction variant that deals with them is worth the additional brain stress. Also, the boatmen bonus is a little unbalancing under the basic rules. However, those are minor issues in a quite good game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Played it only once; it's short and easy to explain, but I need to play it again to judge it properly.  While it has its merits, it seems like it has problems.  For example, it seems like you need to be balanced between getting the various meeple colours to win -- a feature I like very much in E&T, of course.  But you even when you win a hard-fought auction to buy the meeple of your choice, it's often associated with a tile that won't let you play it (due to the island pattern in that row)!  So, it invariably ends up waiting to be placed.  So, then you end up needing lots of grey meeples to place the other ones which couldn't, which wastes lots of auction turns -- one to get the meeple, one to get the grey meeple to use the other one.  (But one grey meeple plus the "canoe" bonus tile allows you to place 5 of different colours, or all of one colour, which is fantastic --> FIGHT for this bonus tile!!!)

At first glance, most important colours:
0) Grey -- depending on how lucky you are with being able to place your other meeples immediately upon purchase.
1) Yellow (at least one early, or you'll suffer during the auctions/acquire meeples phase, and you won't get any bonus tiles for buying the most expensive meeple).
2) Blue -- rarely affected by Viking boats, and each is worth lots of positive points (and lack of negative points) at the end of the game.
3) Black -- protects your other meeples, which is worth points or money.
4) Green and Red -- points, duh!

RATING: N/A
遊戲設置:
1.	玩家一個標記放在得分10位置一個放在自己面前、1個起始板塊
2.	2/3/4人，30/25/20元
3.	72個板塊洗勻分六疊面向下放置在主圖版左方，78個維京人放進束口袋
遊戲規則:
1.	六輪結束，每輪從最左邊開使一片一片開版塊，翻開是島嶼從0開始順時針依序擺放，如果是戰船板塊，從11開始逆時針擺放。
2.	每輪抽12個維京人依照，藍(漁夫)=黃(鐵匠)=綠(斥侯)=紅(貴族)=黑(戰士)=灰(船夫)，從價格0順時針放到11，有少就跳過
3.	順時針每人選一個組合
◎當輪盤上12組配件都被拿光，開始計分：
▲第1、3、5輪計算鐵匠(黃)分數。
▲第2、4、6輪計算漁夫(藍)以外的所有分數。
◎在6輪計分結束後，最後還要計算獎賞分數。
4.	購買板塊
a.支付該格標示金額
b. 價格0的組合僅有在下列兩種情況可以選取：
▲▲價格0的維京人是該顏色的最後一個。
▲輪到玩家時沒有金幣可以支付選取費用，該玩家直接拿取價格0的組合。
c.任何時候可以1分換1元
d.0的板塊被選擇後，會輪盤轉動到對應新的板塊為止。(其他會下降)
5.	放置板塊
a.戰船放置在第一橫排，第一艘戰船必須放置在第一橫列的1~3格內(家園圖
板內)，戰船必須相連
b.島嶼必須與家園版圖或舊有島嶼垂直相接(對角不行)，島嶼有固定方向，不能顛倒。
c.島嶼放置規則與卡卡頌相同，島對島，海對海，若無法放則棄牌，此時維京人放在左上角漁夫符號旁邊。
▲▲當玩家放置第一個戰船或島嶼板塊時，同時也放置起始板塊，玩家可以依據自己的喜好決定兩種板塊的放置順序。
6.	放置維京人
a.若顏色相對應左邊則人放在該板塊，若沒對應放到左上角船夫旁。
b.人放了就不能移動，可以自己把人移到漁夫旁，一島一人。
c.計分錢可以用灰色(船夫)移動人到島嶼。
d.若人前面有戰船且沒有(黑色)戰士保護就會放倒，被威脅!
7.	計分
a. 1、3、5輪結束後，玩家每有1鐵匠在島嶼上，且沒有受到戰船的威脅，可以獲得3金幣。
b. 2、4、6輪)結束後，玩家從起始玩家開始，依順時針順序進行得分和行動，移動分數軌上的標記做紀錄
c. (灰色)船夫: 1個船夫可以移動基地裡同一顏色的所有維京人或每種顏色的維京人各一到空的島嶼板塊上，船夫使用後移出遊戲，第三次計分要全部用
d.戰船: 會威脅下方島上維京人，此威脅會一直延伸到與戰船同顏色的橫排，被威脅的維京人無法得分或獲得金幣。
e. (黑色)戰士: 阻擋威脅，成功阻擋可以獲得站船上分數與金幣
f. (紅色)貴族: 得2分
g.(綠色)斥候: 1分，下方有黃色或藍色各多得1分
h. (黃色)鐵匠: 得三元。
i: (藍色) 漁夫: 遊戲最後餵食。
結束計分後，起始向左傳。
8.最後得分
a.	每一艘未被勇士阻擋的戰船，玩家失去戰船板塊上標示的金幣與分數，如果玩家沒有金幣可以支付，用1分代替1金幣支付
b.	五元換一分
c.	剩下最多船夫(灰色)的得10分，在最後一輪必須用光，除非島嶼已滿或沒有對應的維京人可以放置。
d.	島嶼數量:有最多完整島嶼(未必要有人)得7分，平手共享
e.	島嶼長度:有最長(最多板塊)完成島嶼的玩家獲得5分，平手共享
f.	餵養: 一人須赴一食物，一隻藍色漁夫抵 5份食物，剩餘多得食物一份2分，不足一份扣1分，被戰船威脅或在基地的漁夫無法供應食物。
 遊戲結束，平手筆錢。

進階規則
1.維京人放置到輪盤的方式。
2.船夫規則。
3.特殊板塊的使用。
競標
a起始取13人，起始玩家右手邊的玩家作為拍賣主持人，進行競標。
b.從起始玩家開始，依順時針順序出價，必須高於上一位玩家或PASS。
c.一輪競標，最高得起始，錢給銀行，若沒有則原來起始連任。
d.新的起始玩家，從抽出13個維京人中選擇一個移出遊戲。
e.起始玩家從12個維京人選擇一個顏色，將該顏色的維京人從輪盤11的位置開始往數字低的空格擺放。下一位玩家從剩下的維京人再選一個顏色，然後從剩下格子數字高的空格開始擺放，其餘玩家依此類推，直到維京人都擺放完畢。船夫
◎◎船夫在大計分前一次僅能移動1個維京人到空的島嶼上。
f.特殊板塊:洗牌後分成兩疊，在競標開始前，起始玩家從2疊的特殊板塊翻開4張，面向下放到圖板上對應的格子裡。
◎◎無論何時當玩家拿取輪盤上最貴的組合時，他可以額外獲得1張翻開的特殊板塊。
◎◎當玩家選取價格0的組合時，可以打出板塊並執行功能或置於家園圖板旁待計分時發揮作用。放置在特殊板塊的維京人計分時不算分，且遊戲結束時漁夫也不必對這些維京人進行餵養。

RATING: 6
I don't think Michael Kiesling will ever be my favourite designer in the world, but this effort highlights a problem that I'm increasingly having with Eurogames: Players are dumped into a themeless (What's that wheel supposed to be? Fred Flintstone's dumb waiter?) and fairly chaotic game that has no parallel in reality, then left to sink or swim. All of the options (the characters, landscape and bonus tiles) are presented to the players with no indication of what's coming later, so only short term tactics are possible. Better games have much more manageable levels of randomness.

RATING: 9
Clever auction/buying game with a tenuous grasp of theme.  Ignore the viking link and you have a very clever "reducing price" mechanic in the centre of a game that is very elegant to play.

The game presents many agonising choices and the pain of having to pay 8  for a viking/tile combo makes the game come alive.

There's luck in the order that the tiles come up, but everybody sees the same tiles.  If you don't pay the big money for the tiles you need, then you get left with all the boats and nobody to repel them.

A small, quick game that worth 30 minutes of anybody's time.

RATING: 7
First played June 26th, 2007. Taught in 15min, played in 45 min. This takes a bit to figure out, or so it did for me. The rules take about 15min to explain however it's worth it. It will seem complicated, however it's not as complicated after you get the hang of it. Throw out the first game as a learning/understanding experience. You will need it to figure out the mechanics of the game. The game itself is quick. Once you get in the flow of it, it goes faster. Each player is given a starting area, a start island tile, $25 and 10 VPs. 12 vikings are drawn from the bag and placed in order of (starting at zero) blue, yellow, green, red, black, and grey. Tiles are also placed in the order drawn with island segments at zero going up and ship tiles at 11 going down. The start player is randomally drawn and then each round it passes to the left. You may purchase any viking/tile group at the cost, except the zero tile there are more than one of that color viking (unless you can't pay). You may trade one VP for $1, however not vice versa. Once the zero group is bought, the wheel moves clockwise to make everything a bit cheeper. After purchasing the group you may place the tile if you can in your island area. You must place a tile orthogonal to another one and it must be face up(water/cliff at the bottom). Island tiles mush be place so that it's possible to complete an island with a start and an end. Your starting island tile must be placed with your first tile drawn. The first ship tile may be placed in column 1,2,or 3, however the next must be adjacent to the first and the first three slots must be filled before placing in the 4th column. Once a tile is placed you must either place the viking on that tile(if you can) or place in the homeworld. Boatsman always get placed in the homeworld. Going clockwise, each player buys until all groups have been purchased. You play 6 rounds in the base game. After the odd round you have a small score (only the unthreatened goldsmith produce $3 each) and after each of the even rounds you have a big score (warriors produce ship stated VPs/$ for repelled ships; Nobels produce 2VPs, Scouts produce 1VP for themselves and all goldsmith/fisherman behind them, and Goldsmith produce $3; all of which are unthreatened). If a ship is present in the first space the mast color determines how far down the vikings are threatened. (yellow threatens all except the fisherman) Before Big scoring, you may use your boatsman to move all of one viking type or one of each viking type. Last(6th) round you also have a end game scoring. The most boatsman gets 10VPs, each $5 gold is 1VP, most completed islands is 7VPs and the largest completed island is 5 VPs. All ties, all involved players each recieve the VPs.

RATING: 8.5
Very Euro-feeling game.  Neat wheel mechanic for determining cost of purchases.  The Viking theme isn't really that strong (since there's no combat, looting or plundering), but the game still works very well.

After nearly 50 plays, I finally tried the "Advanced" rules.  [Basic game plays so crisp that I hadn't felt the need for additional rules.]  The advanced rule bonus tiles and modification to the boatsman rules are a neat little twist.  They add some additional considerations to your decision-making process each turn - not completely game altering though.  Glad to try the advanced rules.  May try them again, but am just fine with the basic rules.

After many plays, it's a really good sign that I've seen people win in several different ways.  Note: you're hard pressed to win if you don't get one of the 3 end-game bonuses; however, there is definitely not an automatic "play for this specific bonus and win" sneak-path through the game.

A terrific recent addition to www.yucata.de

RATING: 8
Wikinger manages to avoid the vintage stereotype of raping and pillaging (pat on the back for the designer). But it features horned warriors on the box (slap on the wrist for the artist) and the meeples...look more like Jay Kay than Erik the Red! As for the game itself - the basic game works well enough but it is the advanced version that is the real draw. The mechanics and scoring of the latter are a little fiddly and unintuitive which makes it tricky for a first-timer to figure out suitable tactics (though it helps to have already played the basic game). However, it is worth persevering because Wikinger becomes really interesting when players feel confident enough to splash out on the auction or on purchasing more expensive tiles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
As noted elsewhere, this game would have been a must buy about 6 years ago.  Now, I think it will have a hard time getting through the clatter.  Part of it is the theming.  I am not someone who thinks this is the end-all be all, but if a game is going to stand out for me today, its theme and mechanisms really need to have something to do with one and other.  I realize that Vikings are the flavor of the week in Euroland, but why is it that we are now on the third or fourth Viking game with precious little in the raping and pillaging category?  The "cost rondle" is clever, but should be wedded to an activity with dimminishing costs for the same activity.  Is it really cheaper for the 3rd player to defend himself against Viking long ships?  Perhaps a civilizational game where the rondel represented investments in technology?  Anyway, this is a perfectly fine Eurogame.  If you are new to the hobby you might find it quite clever.  But if you've been in it for a while, you will probably want more horned-helmet for your dollar.

RATING: 7
Initial rating

Finally got to play this and played with 2 players advanced rules [i]including[/i] the auction which I think is important, getting to choose some of the order of the Vikings and getting to buy first are advantages that should be bought (or in 2 player altered I guess, even 3 player would even out BUT depending on which Vikings come out this is more or less important depending).

This is clearly a 2 or 3 player game, sure it works with 4 but the lack of control would not be compelling and of course people that played with 4 will have lower ratings.

Our 2 player game FELT off.  First, there were very few "starting Island" pieces in the first 3 or 4 rounds so of course the 1 or 2 that came out got bought up right away otherwise you would have to throw out most the tiles you bought.  Due to this and also a lack of "gold guys", it seemed we didn't have enough money, after we played one round and both had no money so we actually started the game over but basically had close to the same problem AND if barely any "gold guys" come up, you have barely any money for the next round. You could try to wait for tiles to get cheaper but then you risk losing the one or two critical tiles and/or guys that you need.  Third, barely any grey guys came up rounds 2,3,4 and 5 and with the advanced rules, this means not getting to move barely anyone onto the board unless you get the canoe tiles. 

So as I say, this all felt very "off" during play BUT by the end of the game most things worked out where we both had a good number of islands built and a good number of Vikings on the board so maybe this is just the way the game is I guess? It does seem that this whole balance relies too much on the random draw, I am tempted to make a rules that says at least 2 island start pieces should be up for grabs in round 1 and 2 but I'll leave it for now. (we did take 5 dollars each at start of round 2 and 3 each at start of round 3).  

So right now I am cautiously optimistic as it should be a great little 1 hour middle weight with a ton of scoring things to watch out for and vy for so decisions are interesting throughout (typical of K&K games)

RATING: 5.5
2015-01-14

Initial Rating: 6.0 (January 2015)

It is weird that a game about Vikings doesn't include pillaging but so far I have enjoyed my Vikings' games. When I read the rules and I saw the resemblance to Carcassonne (a game I heavily dislike) I thought I wouldn't like it but I do. 

On the good side, the game plays very fast. You can finish a game under 1 hour with 4 fast players, however it has some interesting decisions (basically which Viking/Tile to purchase, but this decision has many ramifications). The game looks gorgeous and the wheel is great. 

On the bad side, it seems Vikings is expensive for what comes in the box and it is on the light side of the weight game spectrum. Not a bad thing per se but nowadays I prefer heavier games. It has a weak theme (I don't feel it). Sometimes luck of the draw can be overpowering and you cannot get in any way what you planned for. The tiles orientation could be much clearer but this is a minor quibble.

Bottom line, Vikings wasn't what I was expecting (thanks to the name and box art) but it is an ok game to play from time to time. Perhaps one of the best tile laying games I have played and I don't generally like those. For now I want to play again with the progress tiles variant. It is a borderline 6.0 for me.

RATING: 7

I missed a huge rule from the translation because I didn't look closely at the pictures.  Your island tile must touch the mainland OR one of your tiles. RATING: 7.5

REVISION: The more I play the game, the more I like it. I am really enjoying the game more this way, and I'm finding the "set someone else up" aspect to be less with each passing play (more to do with experience, I think, than the mixed basic/advanced rules).  This hybrid of the basic and advanced rules is mentioned on the yellow sheet somewhere, I believe.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Vikings is a popular tile-laying, economic game that has been out for a few years now. Basically players buy sets of tiles and vikings of different kinds and place them - following certain rules - into a grid they have in front of them. Certain vikings only go on certain rows and score differently. The tiles consist of islands, on which vikings are placed, or ships who attack the vikings along columns. If you don't have warrior vikings to protect against the ships you are forced to place, the vikings in that column do not score or generate money. After 6 rounds the game ends. The most innovative part of the game is the pricing wheel. As land/viking combinations are purchased, the prices get lower and lower. Sometimes it is worth paying more for a combo than waiting for the price to drop.

We played with an advanced rule set called the "Progress" version that adds an auction for start player, choices for how to distribute vikings around the wheel, tougher rules for the "boatsman" vikings, and special scoring tiles that can be acquired. I hadn't played THIS version before and I think it is a significant improvement and I don't think I'll ever play the basic rules again. The basic rules get a 6 out of 10 for me, but the Progress rules bring the game up to a 7. A solid play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I took me over half a dozen plays to rate this an eight. There's more to it than is readily apparent, and with experienced players you can finish a game in 35-40 minutes. 

In vikings you lay tiles as your own clan explores farther inland. The warriors defend the coast, the nobleman live nearby, the scouts head inland looking for resources, and the goldsmiths and fisherman provide wealth and food. You purchase tiles that are paired with a "veeple" of one of the above types. The purchasing is actually very interactive, and unlike a lot of other games, there are plenty of times when plunking down a huge amount of cash will not destroy your own position and will sufficiently undercut your opponent's. Great stuff. Good for both casual and heavy gamers. 

Update: First play with the special tiles. Much to my surprise, these add a pleasant layer to an already good game. 

The base game is a heavy-lifter in my collection: always delivers, great decision-making in a short time-frame. The special tiles (without the auction) are gold. After a half-dozen plays with them, I've upgraded my rating to a 9. Never would have expected it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Entirely solid, although after 3 plays I do wonder what this game contributes to the literature overall.  Players acquire both Vikings (in varying colors) and land tiles, using a modified Dutch auction system.  The Vikings provide varying income and VP's depending on their color and placement.  The land tiles create end game scoring opportunities, and also have certain placement rules which will make different ones valuable to different players, depending on how they fit together (beginning, middle and end types), and whether their placement can support a viking at the time.  Additionally, players will necessarily be taking enemy Viking ships which need to be defended against.

I think that the choosing mechanism is interesting but the scoring mechanism is a little mundane and could have used some of the creativity seen in Ra.

The game plays quickly and does have some tension, but there are also times when it plays itself.

[Addendum]  I've now played with the advanced game and I find that the game holds up much better than on first impression.  It gets requested quite often.  I appreciate more now how the need to collect, build, and populate islands is actually quite original and creates some fresh challenges.  The game has become much more than just collecting stuff that scores points.

RATING: 9
The Good: A fun "purchase, then place tiles & meeples" game. The basic game is a little heavier than a gateway game. Plays fairly fast and does not overstay it's welcome. A neat "wheel" mecahanic to rotate purchase costs. The components are very well done. One of the things I love about this game is the wonderful help-icons on the board that keep you from having to go back and look at the rules. Also nice reference cards.

The Bad: Some strategy gamers may feel the basic game is too light after a few playings. However, there is an advanced game, which adds more strategy. In the basic game, the mix of tiles and meeples that comes out seems to vary wildly, giving each game a completely different feel. This creates some games where money is VERY tight, other games where you have an excess. Some may like this, but I found that this caused some games to be way too easy, with fewer meaningful decisions.

RATING: 6
When I think of a Viking game, I expect to figuratively chew on my shield, swing my virtual axe and feel the sea spray in my face.  However, Wikinger/Vikings is a clever Euro-game.

Wikinger offers an interesting puzzle to work on. You need to do multiple things right to get the highest score. The motiff is all about Vikings - raiding boats, defending your shores, etc. For me, the lack is that the activities of the game are divorced from the game's theme. You evaluate how much gold to spend, which shaped islands you need, which types of Vikings you need, and whether you'll be helping the next player too much by failing to do something.

Wikinger/Vikings' game mechanics are good. The price wheel is innovative. Overall the game is really quite clever.  If you like Euro-games, I suspect Wikinger/Vikings may be a game you will enjoy.  I will just have turn to Fire & Axe for my virtual shield biting.

A novel game board with tile buying at changing prices. Do you pay top dollar for the tile you want or risk other players buying it first if you wait for it to become cheaper? End of game bonuses add to the tactics and influence tile priority.

Played a 2-player progress version, but without the auction system (which seemed over-complicated on paper). Really enjoyed the bonus tiles and the trade-off in generally having to buy a boat to get them.

RATING: 10
Wow! I enjoyed this game more than any other after one play. It was simply awesome! The game is well-balanced, offers many paths to victory and has some really neat mechanics. I had VERY high expectations and it still somehow exceeded them. A solid 10!

Update 4/12/14: This is still my favorite game. I'm not saying it's the most innovative or impressive game design, but what it does, it does so well. Auction/bidding, worker placement, resource management and tile laying blend so well together in this gem. I am extremely pleased that Z-Man is reprinting this game. I don't know if there is anything to improve on, but I'm excited to buy a second copy.

Update 1/21/15: Still the best. Played with Erin last night (2 player) in just under an hour. It came down to money, and I won by one point--such a close game! She's certainly has some AP in this game, so with experienced players at that count, I think you can knock a game out in 45 minutes easily. We hadn't played in about 9 months, but didn't have any trouble remembering the rules. I can't say that about most games. Of the games I own, Vikings might have the best rule set to decision-making ratio. The saving grace, and what keeps it from bogging down, is that it's quite tactical, especially as you add players. I think players sense that and know that they just need to make the best possible decision on their current turn. I love how the board is small, but functional. I love the art, however simple it may be. The money in this game was nice before, with cardboard tokens of different colors and sizes. But, of course, I added metal barbarian coins to the game, which just makes it that much better and tactile. It's hard to have a favorite game. I love so many games. I think it's the perfect blend of mechanisms that allows this one to rise over the others.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Don't let the name Vikings fool you!  You will not be fighting.  You will not be conquering.  You will not, in fact, be doing anything you might expect from a game called Vikings.  You will essentially be creating a grid of little tiles to get points.  No, really.

The so-called Vikings theme is totally pasted on.  It has nothing to do with the mechanics of the game.  In the game you are buying tiles to put into your tableau.  Depending on how you arrange the tiles you will score more or less points.  It could have been called Spreadsheet: The Game.

Some people love Euros.  Great for them.  I really mean it.  However, the more I play the more I don't get the appeal.  Doing abstract things to generate points, with little to no theme, doesn't get me interested at all.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Let me preface this by saying we only played the base game, and only once at that.

I expected Vikings to be a middle weight game, but it played like a light filler. The luck factor seemed quite high, as the fixed turn order often dictated how many boats you'd have to take in a round. I'm not sure I like the idea of the gray meeples. It seems like it's too easy to move your other colors down later if you snag one of those. And since they are paired with boats often, the benefit of them balances with the detriment of having a boat. The blue meeples also seem quite swingy. In a 3-player game, each player will have perhaps 22 Vikings to feed at game end. If you have 4 blues, you lose 2 points. If you have 5 blues, you get 6 points. So that last blue is worth 8 points and each additional blue is worth 10 points. This seems to necessitate taking blues early, and since they are cheapest, early turns seem scripted. Given how well the game is rated, I must be missing some subtleties and buying more expensive tiles early must have merit.

I hear the advanced game has an auction for turn order and advanced tiles, so I will reserve final judgment until playing with those rules.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
The more I play Vikings, the more I enjoy it. In it's base form there is enough randomness to make it enjoyable for a group spanning multiple skill levels, and the advanced game adds just enough to make the decisions fresh and interesting. Easily my go-to game to start a game day or play with a varied group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Really nice tile laying game, where tiles and vikings are paired up at a rondell-type market, where the price drops as the cheapest tiles are claimed (which can only happen if the viking is the only one left of its colour or the player grabbing it cannot afford to buy any other tile).

You are building up a series of islands and inhabiting them with your vikings, to earn you money, points, to fight enemy ships and to feed the rest of your clan. If you play with the advanced rules you also have bonus tiles that can be claimed if you buy the most expensive tile on the market. The bonus tiles could help during the game or score you extra points at the end of the game.

Fairly easy to get into, and very enjoyable.

RATING: 5.5
(Rating for basic game) Well, the mechanics are different, I'll give it that. But new mechanics does not an interesting game make (look Ma! Like Yoda I speak :)). The mechanics of the warriors/boats wis kind of hockey at best -- and you can end up with totally unwanted tiles just by being stuck at the wrong end of the turn order. More of a solitaire game than anything -- it's difficult to evaluate each player's positions and what tile/viking they might want, and so therefore best played as a tactical game, maximizing your own score without worrying too much on how the board position will change before your turn come again. Overall, a nice effort, I'd like to play again, but it feels more like a mix of good ideas with mixed results than a good game.

RATING: 8
A tough and somewhat unique buying/placement game that's much heavier than it looks.  The purhasing wheel is really what sets this apart from other games.  Players are tying to buy a good combination of viking & land tile without leading their opponents to purchases that would help them too much.  Certain purchases will make the costs for future purchases cheaper.  It can really be quite tactical and players will want to diversify and reduce pirate raids but it's not that easy for the right cost.  Overall the game can be somewhat tactical at times and it's very much a euro.  The viking theme works but don't expect any player vs. player fighting or pillaging.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A game with very neat mechanics that require players to balance many factors against each other, yet still manages to have very simple decisions (i.e., on their turn literally the only thing a player can do is buy a viking and an island tile).

The game revolves around purchasing vikings and islands; you can put vikings on islands, which ends up netting you victory points.  The trouble comes, of course, in that you need specific island tiles with specific vikings on them, and they rarely are available at the ideal time.

The mechanism for purchasing island tiles and vikings is very cool: island tiles and vikings are semi-randomly paired up, and their price is determined by a wheel (which results in changing prices depending on what people have purchased).  I find the balancing of needs to be difficult, and was horribly beaten in my first two games.

The mechanics are very cool, and the game length is short enough to allow it to fit nicely into a long series of gaming.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Each player is attempting to build up their viking empire. There are 6 turns in the game. Each turn, there are 12 vikings/ships and 12 lands for purchase. The 12 vikings are drawn at random from a bag, while the land is also randomly drawn. The unique mechanism in this game is that purchases are done on a wheel,allowing for fluctuating purchases depending what is available. Players score for different types of vikings: fisherman, goldsmiths, scouts, nobles and warriors. Players also score for completed islands. 

This is a fun fast paced game. The wheel mechanism is a fairly new mechanism and works here. There are different strategies to try, such as concentrating on nobles, goldsmiths or fisherman. What does bother me a bit is that with 6 rounds, and with 4 players, not everyone will get a chance to go first. So potentially you lose out on a fisherman because of turn order. Still, it's fun to play, and there are some things beyond your control such as when you are getting your last tile, you may not have much  of a choice or decision.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Quite an interesting mix of mechanics in this game.  There are different colored Vikings that provide you with different bonuses that you choose in an interesting way.  You cannot take the cheapest Viking of one color unless it is the last one, so you are constantly worried about what you are leaving your opponents.  

After selecting a Viking he needs to be placed on the islands that you also have selected earlier in the round.  It is important how you place your Vikings because depending on what is in front or behind your Viking he scores differently.  If you do not have a properly placed land mass, it is possible you will not be able to place that Viking where you wanted.

The game is very interesting and every game has me thinking pretty deeply, especially about what you are leaving your opponents.

RATING: 8
About the basic game: I liked the game. You can get quite (un)lucky with the auctioning wheel depending how occupations are divided but most of the time it feels you can make meaningful decisions and control your game. Rules are clear and playing is straight forward. 

About advanced game: You really should play the advanced game if you're playing with other than beginners. Starting players auctions make it possible to control a bit more of your fate. Advanced game is quite a bit more nasty as you can normally only take one person with a boatsman onto your tiles. I definitely like game more with these set of rules. 

Game works with 2-4 players equally well. With less players in the game you gain more tiles during the game and game is more forgiving. When playing with 4 players it can get quite nasty as you're all competing for same resources more. Either way it's a very good game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Vikings gives a bit of a twist to the usual ingredients of German family games of the fleshier kind - literally! Its most distinctive feature is a wheel indicating the prices of the usual drafted goods (here it's pairs of tiles and figures in any combination of colours and shapes, respectively); when the pair for free is drafted the wheel turns until there is a new one and the prices of all pairs sink accordingly.

Otherwise Vikings still manages to feel both fresh and solid without being necessarily heavy - as with other games of its kind all the interaction lies in drafting properly, but since players still have to arrange the chosen tiles and figures in a single pattern and get enough points and money to keep going, the decisions to take are seldom trivial.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
[b]Vikings[/b] was reacquired* 11/14/15 then sold via auction 4/8//16. 

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I've enjoyed quite a few Kramer/Kiesling designs and Kiesling knocked this one out of the park on his own. I enjoyed the game even more once we added the expansion/bonus tiles. It took a game that could begin to feel a bit samey in terms of strategies and really opened things up. You never know which bonus opportunities will come up when so a lot of times those first few buildings can really help give you a focus. Money also becomes a very viable way to score a bunch of points if you manage to get those multipliers. 

I would definitely recommend checking out Vikings, it plays well with 2, makes a great next step family game and with the inclusion of the expansion tiles (which come in the box) offers a deeper game play experience and on top of that it wraps up in 60 minutes or less.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- Plays great with 2 and is a great family game.
- Tile laying: one of my favorite mechanics in games especially when it is not the [i]only[/i] mechanic.
- The market wheel: Innovative mechanic that determines how much a certain tile/veeple set is going to cost. 
[i]This creates a lot of tense moments when you try to determine whether you can wait one more round to get the tile/veeple you need for a lower price.[/i]
- Bonus tiles add more strategy to the game without adding a lot of new rules or added complexity.[/i]

*Was originally received in a math trade (11/10/14) and then traded away in a math trade 3/17/15.

RATING: 6.85
This game really feels like a sea faring carcassonne in a way, but with new point collecting strategies! This game is great! A nice medium weight game you can play on a week night without being overwhelmed. Lay out the tiles around the wheel and set the vikings in color order around the tiles. Each colored viking offers you different perks, gold, protection, food, etc. Each tile can be connected to other tiles to create a sea of islands. Try to keep your vikings on the islands or move them all by using the boatmen!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Topshelf lightweight Euro, full of hard choices and tradeoffs with every turn. The turning price wheel is a wonderful addition to the gaming scene. Each turn you need to assess who wants the remaining tiles and whether the tiles you really want will still be there by your next turn or not, and that continual assessment drives the game along. There's also a tradeoff in going for money (invest in your engine) or secure long term VP's. A whole bunch of rich gaming decisions in a shortish but complete and satisfactory timeframe.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Definitely an interesting game, where you must choose your tile/veeple pair vs. cost, plus what it will leave for your opponent, plus how to construct your islands. The rules are probably a 2.5, and depth of play makes it a bit heavier. It is more tactical than strategic, as you basically have to do the best you can with the tiles/veeples available.

Although you don't have too many decisions, there could be some AP as you try to think multiple moves ahead, including blocking your opponent(s). Definitely some opportunities for screwage, although not too nasty. However, it is a BRUTAL competition for scarce resources, like dogs fighting in an alley over food scraps. There are a couple opportunities each game to grab something you don't need just to deny your opponent.

I don't really want to play again because it's kind of stressful for me. I prefer games with more indirect interaction. As a result, even though it is a very well-designed game, I'm dropping my rating from 7 to 6.

RATING: 8
Great, original little game, unlike anything else I've played. There's enough strategy, looking ahead and watching your opponents to keep your decisions interesting in what is otherwise a simple and light game. The rules for tiles distribution make for changing and fresh situations. Gameplay is reactive for a large part, though; you have to make the most of the hand you're dealt (not literally).

Might be too light (too little control) for gamers, and too many rules (each different colored viking scores differently, and how and when you can bid and place isn't superintuitive) for non-gamers, but otherwise a very solid game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Though I infrequently play with gaming groups I know this design has been getting a lot of play, and eventually I managed to give Vikings a go. I can see why it is so popular: its solitaire Carcassonne with some strategy. 

The money wheel provides some look ahead and interesting consequences when buying plots of land and Vikings; one consequence of buying pricey real estate is a rule warping power tile. Plots are placed in your own yard ala Carc with a puzzle feel like Princes of Florence, though thankfully for some not so spatial. Vikings must be placed with the plot or later elsewhere in your area "paratrooper" style. 

So another vp/money generating engine with many (variable) avenues to advance, but what I like about this design is that it seems that the engine block is made of glass (and I don't feel the need to read a manual as the icons are almost self explanatory): i.e. I can SEE what is happening that is going to be productive, I know I need to get 5 multi coloured Vikings in a row to maximise, but it ain't going to happen so easily. 

Sadly, when playing Vikings, Led Zep's "Immigrant Song" doesn't readily come to mind (like it does when playing Fire and Axe); in fact the meeples make me think of Jamiroquai.

RATING: 4
This game plays well. You get this tile + meeple selection system similar to Seeland. Then you place the tile + meeple in a row on your board in a way similar to Hawaii. Sometimes you are forced to take ship tiles, which hurt your column depending on the color of the ship and if you have a protection meeple there or not. Really, the game is fine, it just isn't different enough from other games I own that I like more. The way the meeples are randomly pulled from the bag can sometimes be frustrating. It's not uncommon to be ruined by luck of the draw here, and the game doesn't provide any catch-up ability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Yet another Euro with a weak theme, not much player interaction, complex scoring to ensure a "balanced" game and uncreative strategies, and emphasis on quick tactical decisions over long-term planning.

It has the feel of Alhambra/Palazzo -- you draft tiles, pay for them, then add those tiles to your personal display and arrange in such a way to maximize VP (and in this game) income.

Despite its uninspiring nature, it does play quickly, Unlike Alhambra, which I cannot tolerate, you're never left in a situation in which you are totally screwed; the frustration factor is much, much lower.

The theme has nothing to do with the game. Actually, it would probably make sense if this were a city building game. The supply-demand mechanism I could live with theme-wise. I honestly believe any attempt to implement a theme with this game is hopeless. The problem is what you're trying to build with the tiles: You're trying to arrange tiles and meeples on a grid so that they score horizontally and vertically. Really, the only thing that could be organized like that in the real world is an Excel spreadsheet. Even cities don't operate in rowas and columns; they tend to grow and flourish by clumps of irregular sized neighbourhoods. Simply look at some of the island/viking meeple pictures here; could you imagine a viking colony as orderly and organized as that? Different types of vikings all in neat rows?

It seems like the best strategy is to ensure you have a stable income, then go after points. This is similar to many games, but in this case, it's actually easy to secure a stable income. After that, you simply have to pay attention to what tiles and vikings your opponents want and try to take them before they do, But most tiles can be used effectively anyway. These two things -- income generation and a surplus of pretty good tiles undoubtedly reduce the frustration factor (as in Alhambra) but dramatically reduce the tension.

Gameplay:

The advanced tiles and auction variants are interesting. These variants change the strategy quite a bit, but overall make the auction system much tighter.

RATING: 8
A very interesting game with unique mechanics. It has an auction element (advance game) to it along with strategic tile placement to maximize points. Great player interaction as well as you try to anticipate what each other might do. 

Different vikings with varying advantages and land tiles are placed around a wheel. The wheel determines how much things cost and is continually changing. One must figure out how to manipulate the wheel to acquire the land tile and viking of their choice and block others from getting their choice. The 2 and 3 player games are great. Haven't tried with 4.

A solid fun game that plays in about 45 minutes once experienced.

Wikinger, c'est un matos joli, un thème un peu plaqué mais pourquoi pas, plein de règles dans tous les sens (et des dizaines de sources de points de victoire), pour un résultat plutôt confus, peu interactif, hasardeux et au final peu emballant. Il me fait vraiment l'impression d'un énième jeu « à l'allemande », dans le sens le plus classique du terme.

Le coup de la roue est pas mal trouvé, ça donne une sorte d'intermédiaire entre le jeu d'enchères et le blocage à la [gameid=9217]. Ça me fait aussi un peu penser au paiement des architectes aux [gameid=24480]. Malheureusement, l'idée aurait gagné à être enrobée dans un jeu un peu plus fun.

Bref, pas un jeu mauvais, mais un jeu "correct" et tout à fait dispensable. À envisager si vous avez déjà épuisé les [gameid=24480], [gameid=25554], [gameid=9217], [gameid=481], [gameid=2476], [gameid=13642]... et la centaine d'autres bons jeux « allemands tendance poids moyen » qui existent sur le marché.

(PS. : je n'y ai joué qu'à quatre joueurs, et il semblerait que le jeu soit plus intéressant à deux ou trois.)

RATING: 7.5
分數
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
Vikings is strategic tile-based game where players spend gold to buy both a viking and an island tile, and place them in their tableau to their best advantage.  The game board includes a score track, a round track, and the payment wheel; the payment wheel is one of the central mechanics of the game.  The wheel has 12 slots, numbered 0 - 11, each corresponding with a location where a tile and viking combo will rest.  The number of the slot determines the cost for that set.  There are two types of tiles in the game, islands and ships, and six different types of vikings.  Each player also receives a player base.  The game plays over 6 rounds, then a final scoring round occurs and the victor is determined.

When the game is setup all the tiles are shuffled then arranged into stacks of 12 tiles, then placed on the round track.  At the beginning of each round the next tile stack is removed from the round track and distributed around the payment wheel.   Islands are start at 0 and work up to 11 while ships start at 11 and work down to 0.  Finally 12 vikings are chosen from a cloth bag and arranged on the offered tiles, always in the same color order.  Players take turns buying the tiles until there are no titles left to purchase.  A player must buy a tile, and may buy any tile he can afford, the only exception being the tile priced at 0, which can only be purchased when no other vikings of the same color are available, or the player cannot afford any of the offered tiles.  When the tile at 0 is purchased the wheel is rotated clockwise until another tile is worth 0.  When a tile is purchased it is immediately placed in the player's play area; however, the tile must be orthogonally adjacent to another tile or the the player base.  Any island tiles must be played correctly orientated, and the tile's edges must match those it is adjacent to (water to water and land to land), otherwise the tile is discarded from play.  All ship tiles are played in the first row of the player's tableau.

Each viking color corresponds to a particular fashion of scoring in the game, though wile every tile will be played, not every viking will come into play.  Each row in the player's tableau corresponds to one of the vikings, and if an island is placed in the same row as the viking that came with the tile, then the viking can be placed on the tile just played, otherwise he's set on the player's base.  Vikings score when they are on islands, with the only exception being the boatsmen, who score vikings on the base.

The game has a fascinating depth of strategy, as players not only must choose which tiles they want, weighed against which vikings they want, they must also manage the payment wheel. Careful selection of the tile/viking combo will reward the player, and skillful management of the payment wheel can force an opponent to take a disadvantageous tile/viking set.  Vikings is a marvelous game of strategy and dynamics.

Update: After more than a hundred plays I do feel that the only particular strike against this game is the value of oversupply of fishermen. Oversupply value should be halved to one instead of two points and it would still be worth picking up every one you can get your hands on.
