RATING: 6
In the family of games where you draft tiles and arrange them on your mat, Vikings is right up there with Factory Fun and Thurn and Taxis. I am impressed with the strong, elegant puzzle feel that it exudes simply from square tiles and colored meeples (unlike the complete travesty that is Maori).

The supply track adjustment for tile/meeple combo is a little weird (the supply track itself and the rules for placing combos works well), and seems like an ill-fitting way to fit in the coolness of rotating the wheel, at the cost of introducing some weird gameplay. Just seems like you can really game it (I take this, so you have to take that, so I get to take this), especially in 2-player. I guess that just means money is a lot more important with 2 players. Still, I think a gentler sliding scale (move combos down the scale to replace ones that were drafted) would have fitted better.

This game is the poster child for Euros with an awesome-sounding name and box cover, then you play the game and find the theme is a disappointment. Thought you were going to raid islands and sail the high seas? Sucka, go sit in the corner and work on your puzzle.

RATING: 7.5
I have to say that the most exciting thing about the game is that there is an innovative new market system.  The rest of the game is rather ok.  After digesting the rules I was questioning whether it was going to work or not, but it is a successful design.

Mostly you are optimizing your points and giving yourself options for placement in the future.  Just as with many other Euros most of the "interaction" here is in drafting items you can use that other players need.  Unlike something like St. Petersburg, the reprocusions are more dramatic and you must constantly balance what will be left for you if you screw someone else.  

What makes the game worthwhile is that each of the drafts have two different features, the job of the viking and the land tile.  There are times where it is absolutely worth taking a tile when you don't want the man, especially when it will allow for more options later in the round.  Knowing when you are going to get free guys/tiles and preparing for their placement is essential in this game as the money is so tight.  There is also a satisfaction in how you build your tiles.

The market has the potential to be very harsh and if you pass up opportunities you may end up getting humped into an untenable position.  Still though, it is engaging, and surprisingly more interactive that is has rights to be.

UPDATE: The game is much improved by the advanced game.  The decisions are more difficult and the options to get the special tile by buying the most expensive item makes for more play options, and makes for more decision making.

Still, it can be a harsh game.  If things go wrong for you, it is tough to get back in.

UPDATE: This game gets better with more plays.  I highly recommend ONLY playing this with the advanced rules.

RATING: 6
This is one of those games where I completely respect its design and consider it a great game and can see why many people love it...while also not being one of those people.  It's just simply not for me or my taste of game although because it is so good of a game I wouldn't hate myself for playing it.

The game is a simple drafting and tile-placement affair.  The Vikings theme is completely inconsequential, this is a very abstract game (which should fill you in on why I don't care for it since I'm not a fan of most abstract titles).  Each round several tiles depicting one of the three pieces of an island (left, middle, right) or a boat are placed randomly around a rondel.  On these islands are randomly placed vikings of several different colors.  The rondel shows increasing prices for drafting both the tile/viking pairs.  When the front of the rondel is empty the rondel rotates lowering the prices of the remaining slots.

After drafting these tile/viking pairs you place the tile along your player board building islands and placing vikings in the correctly colored rows which score you points or earn you money in different ways during the scoring rounds.  The actual game comes in which pairs you draft and where you place them along your player board since island pieces can only go where they fit and vikings only stick around on islands when they are placed in the correct rows.

Like I said, this is very well designed.  It plays very smoothly and is also accessible and reasonably short even at max player count.  But even as a filler it's just not something that appeals to me.  For me, after one play I've seen everything this game has to offer.  The decisions came easily to me and I didn't feel like there were many interesting decisions to be made from round to round.  There wasn't enough tension in the competition for tiles and without that there is certainly no theme or engaging gameplay to keep me interested.  It is just kind of bland...again, for me.

+ Different game depending on the number of players
+ Great randomizer with the tiles and vikings draw
DISLIKE
- No equal number of round during 4 player games makes it flawed
- Changing VP into cash; if you don't have the cash, it should be too bad for you
VERDICT
Plays quick, lots of strategy, different paths to victory, and a different game every time.
This is one of the best under 1 hour 3 player game.
When you are familiar with the base game, play the progress version to completely renew the game.
My ultimate 3 players 1-hour game!

RATING: 8.5
Comments for Vikings with special tiles, no bidding, mostly 2 p :

Vikings is a tough one.  I did not like it at first and could not see the strategy.  I think I took it as a lighter-than-it-is family game.  Which it is not.  There is quite a bit of forward planning and spatial organization necessary for doing well. There are plenty of ways to grab little bits of points here and there, not including the nice end game bonuses.  

It's one game where I feel a sense of dread come over me mid-game when I know I have not done well.  It can be surprising, though, to find I was within 1-3 points of winning. Which means it is somewhat difficult to track your standing.  That's not a bad thing just intriguing.

Fun Factor : :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:

Fulfillment Factor : :star::star::star::star::nostar:

@Work
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
If you like Carcassone you'll also like Vikings.

I actually like it better than Carcassone (I always play w/Inns and Cathedrals, no more, no less) so far.

Comparison:

It has the same feel and mechanics as Carc, but it is more "gamey".  Yes luck plays some role in Vikings, but not to the same extant as Carc.  Not even close.

The playing time is similar.

For me Vikings is equally fun with 2-4 players.  I think Carc is best with 2 and gets worse as you add more players.

Components are similar.

I do think Carc is more friendly to newbies and children, but Vikings isn't that much more difficult to grasp.  Any adult who is not afraid of bord games will completely grasp the game after 1 play.

Carc is prettier in the end.  You do not end up with a beutiful layout of cities, roads and countryside. You just end up with two sets of linear archipeligos.  But the artwork is nice.  

I love the Gold Wheel!  It is auction without bidding.  The problem with auctions is that newcomers have no idea of valuation, yet auctions do even out luck.  The perfect middle ground is a Gold Wheel.  Value is much easier to grasp for beginners, but luck has much less influence for gamers.  It is clever and may well be a mechanic seen many more tile-laying games. 

Vikings also comes with an expansion (about 20 additional tiles and a rule sheet) for more advanced play.  I haven't even glanced at this yet so I cannot comment on the play.  I can say, that it is pretty cool that the expansion is included in the base game.
It is something they could have charged $8-$10 for later as the base game is very good on it's own, but they threw it in. Very nice.

I still have a place in my collection for Carcassone, but if I had to choose between the two, I'd pick Vikings.

Update:  Rating stays the same. Still love that gold wheel.

  


Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Just an old fashioned euro.  Neat little tidy package.  Fun to look at the configuration and figure out all of the nifty moves tucked away.  The coin bits are just a treat.  Probably my favorite of all cardboard currency. I expect this to rise with more plays.  These valuation games can be tricky the first couple of plays, and my geekbuddies are pretty consistent with its depth.  I haven't played the "progress" version, but I"m saving it until its clearly necessary.  Timing, valuation, and a clean rule set.  This is one of my sweet spots and it hits it quite well.  I'll revisit this comment after 8 plays, but I think 7 is my minimum.  

The rule of not being able to ake a 0 column unless its the last of the viking type makes the decisions in the game so much more juicy.  Plus with the standard 2 player game, the conflict over maximizng goldsmiths and fisherman but at the expense at giving your opponent first shot at cheaper warriors is the crux of every round.  

I would call this game carcassone with shopping.

Five games plays out the game out of the box.  The progress version from here on out for me.  Wow, the multiple paths to victory just exploded out of the game.  The added decision points, and there is just so much to consider.  I am thinking this game is deeper than it appears.  The special power tiles are a must.  The added choice of paying more with a special  power, vs. opening a cheap special power for your opponent adds to a tricky valuation as it is.  Plus, it helps open up skewed strategies.  Good good times.  I think the "progress variant" without the auction adds two points on the rating of the game, breathes so much life and replayability into the game. I am glad I played the "base" game for a few plays as the nuance was easier to pick up. Probably after many plays, the auction will be added, as it will be easier to value what going first means. 

The game forces you to deal with unique situations constantly, and makes it tricky to price items.  The boats may come out way before warriors.  Warriors may constantly be tossed back into the box, make the player vulnerable.  A steady stream of boatsman enhancements may come out, making the competition for most unique., etc.  The adapting to the situations that come up, and the shifting value of the special tiles etc.  Anyways, this game is tricky and underrated.

RATING: 8.4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Surprisingly fresh German-style game, and a solo outing from Michael Kiesling (of Kramer & Kiesling fame, e.g., Tikal, Maharaja, Torres). I'd overlooked the game when it first came out because it looked like just another boring game with recycled mechanics and a pasted on theme, but I was wrong. A friend convinced me to give it a try, and I quickly went out to purchase a copy later that week. The "advanced rules" are what make the game good and I recommend skipping right to those if you have any board gaming experience. There are loads of interesting decisions to make in this game and a plethora of scoring options that have kept me coming back for 10 plays so far, and hopefully many more to come.

Purchased: January 4, 2009

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[thing=27173][/thing] is a somewhat light tile laying game, in which the players buy tiles in order to form their village. However, vikings (ships) will come and deactivate the meeples in the tiles if they aren't protected by a warrior (black) meeple.

The meeples do things accordingly to their color:

- Black - protects the tiles from vikings;
- Red - gives victory points;
- Yellow - gives gold;
- Blue - feeds the meeples;
- Green - gives a benefit to all other meeples (except the black);
- Grey - takes meeples from the reserve to put in the tiles.

The ships, though they can be a problem, also give one of two things: victory points or money.

When a player buys a tile he must add it somewhere, respecting the tile laying rules. Every tile comes with a meeple, and if the row the player adds the tile is the same of that meeple, he can leave the meeple in the tile, otherwise the meeple goes to the reserve.

Players get points by the most completed islands, by the largest island, by meeples and ships, by grey unused meeples, and lose points by meeple that aren't feed.

[thing=27173][/thing] is a smart game, one that deserves your attention if you like this sort of easy going game, that still requires thought and has depth.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
This has been on my radar due to the pricing wheel, but having watched a good video on it I'm not sure I need to own it.

The pricing wheel matches a price to a pairing of a tile (island or ship) or a meeple (of varying colors). The population of the wheel isn't completely random as ships are sorted above (i.e. in price) island tiles and the colors have a sorting order as well. You cannot take the free option unless that's the only way to get that color of meeple, and if you do take it, this triggers the price drops for the other players (so that there is always a free option).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Rating for 2 player games with special action tiles. Pretty meaty Euro-style optimization game that plays very well with two. One of the few games with an auction mechanic that is enjoyable with that number of players, and it achieves it without the need for an artificial "neutral player". The original (and pretty straightforward) market wheel mechanic is brilliant. Rules are pretty simple, although the sheer number of scoring opportunities can seem a little daunting at first. In the base game, it is a bit unforgiving: It can be difficult to compensate for any mistakes made in the first two rounds. This does change with the introduction of the special tiles however. If you, like me, love to explore a game's mechanics, than this could be a good game for you. And it does not take more than 1 to 1.5 hours to play. Forget about the theme though - this game might as well have been about assets. Still, the little Viking meeples are very nice :-).

RATING: 7
This is a terrible Viking game. I think the only ones getting pillaged are your own villages. Maybe "Invaders!" would have been a better title.

That aside, the game is fantastic. Players take turns buying a random pairing of a tile and a colored meeple. A very clever pricing wheel changes the market value of the various pairings any time someone takes the free offering. After that, it's just a very simple tile laying game. But that's the beauty, you can explain this game in 5 minutes flat. Yet the pricing wheel provides a ton of game space, planning and push your luck elements. It's way more fun than the sum of its parts, as is true of most classic Euros.

RATING: 7.75
Tile laying plus island building equals population growing, point-producing happiness.  Very under-appreciated here on the 'Geek, Vikings is an exceedingly well designed Michael Kiesling game.

Don't allow any one to buffalo you into believing this is multi-player solitaire.  I can't count the number of times I wanted to cry as an opponent nabbed a tile I dearly coveted.  

You read that right--I said, "Nabbed."  Though not 'technically' correct (because the tile laid itself bare on the open market) It. Was. Mine. 

There are enough variabilities to keep this game fresh for a long--enjoyably long--time.  Building islands, then populating them with various skilled colonists, and protecting them from nasty viking-laden ships to score big points is a joy.  Must remember to employ fisherman too, as those colonists insist on eating.

Bonus points at the end of the game keep the winner secret until they are finally tallied.  I like this element in a game.  Keeps every one on their toes throughout the match.

Easy to learn as well as teach, an advanced game that allows people to grow into a richer, deeper experience of gaming, and it is pleasurable to watch your island collection grow.

Family fun, gamer friendly.  :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Vikings has often been accused of not being very thematic, which fair enough. The theme could have been pretty much anything, but I do enjoy how adorable the little viking meeples are.

The rondel and tile-laying aspects of the game really work for me. Choosing both a tile and a meeple with the same action provides an interesting choice. If I can find a spot where I want both, excellent! If I can't, would I rather go for the tile or the meeple I want? How much am I willing to spend? All this while managing money to ensure I can get what I want when I want it as well as building up my different meeple types in each row to get bonuses. Vikings also scales amazingly well. I enjoy it equally at 2, 3, and 4 players and it plays pretty quickly at all player counts.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
Autopilot drafting meets personal jigsaw puzzle. Blue and yellow vikings are irrefutably the best, and just in case you needed a push in that direction the pieces always start on the cheap side of the wheel. Yellow vikings provide moolah for much-needed purchasing power and/or end-game VP's while blue vikings only get better in quantity and as the game progresses because overfeeding is worth 8 points - more than a red counted in every round and one point shy of a perfect green. Third strike is that a green's value is contingent upon accompanying yellow and blue, practically solidifying the draft lineup of yellow/blue => black/green => red/ship, meaning the outcome boils down to favorable terrain acquisition and amounts of blues/yellows per round (always better for first player to pull totals of 4, 5, 7, 8, 10, or 11 because he can snag the extra), which of course is decided by bag-pulling randomness. Hard not to laugh at viking islands segregated by color + black meeples though. Remember in Puerto Rico where colonists from the slave ship were black? That was awesome.

RATING: 8
I'd kind of ignored this game as a Viking game with no pillaging sounded odd. But when it got a reprint I looked into it a bit more and it looked perfect for where my taste in gaming is running lately (fairly simple to explain, not hard to set up/ take down, still provides lots of interesting decisions) and it is!  Feeling out the right time to buy more expensive tiles or to take the zero and make everything cheaper for everyone else balanced with developing your islands so you can get your tiles in place and your meeples working as quickly as possible makes this an excellent medium weight Euro.

Note:  I generally play between the regular and advanced game.  We leave out the auction but do include the bonus tiles.

RATING: 6
Unique purchasing decisions combining the island tile and the type of meeple that comes with it.  I also like how scoring is affected by the interaction between the different vikings, some of it was dependent on what kind you have and how they are placed.  What I didn't care for was how starting player always have the advantage and there was no way to mitigate this, nor to change the turn order besides it being passed to the next person each round.  Therefore, it really came down to how lucky you are in the drawing of the tiles and the vikings when you get the initial selection.  Not to mention there is an odd number of phases, so essentially the very first player would get this benefit twice in the game compared to everyone else.
